6/27/23, 19:24 - Venkataraman: import numpy as np
grid_world = np.array([[0, 0, 0],
                      [0, 1, 0],
                      [0, 0, 2]])
q_table = np.zeros((3, 3, 4))
learning_rate = 0.1
discount_factor = 0.9
num_episodes = 10

for episode in range(num_episodes):
    state = (0, 0)  # starting state
    while state != (2, 2):  # while the goal is not reached
        # Choose an action based on the epsilon-greedy policy
        if np.random.rand() < 0.1:
            action = np.random.randint(4)  # random action
        else:
            action = np.argmax(q_table[state])

        # Take the action and observe the next state and reward
        next_state = tuple(np.add(state, [(0, -1), (0, 1), (-1, 0), (1, 0)][action]))

        # Check if the next state is valid or out of bounds
        if next_state[0] < 0 or next_state[0] >= grid_world.shape[0] or next_state[1] < 0 or next_state[1] >= grid_world.shape[1]:
            # Out of bounds, assign a negative reward and stay in the current state
            reward = -1
            next_state = state
        else:
            reward = -1 if grid_world[next_state] == 0 else 10

        # Update the Q-value for the current state-action pair
        q_table[state][action] += learning_rate * (reward + discount_factor * np.max(q_table[next_state]) - q_table[state][action])

        state = next_state  # move to the next state

    # Print the learned Q-table
    print(q_table)

6/27/23, 21:04 - Venkataraman: import pandas as pd
from sklearn.datasets import load_iris
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier
from sklearn.metrics import confusion_matrix, accuracy_score

dataset = load_iris()

x, y = pd.DataFrame(dataset.data), pd.DataFrame(dataset.target)

x_train, x_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    x, y, test_size=.33, random_state=32)

model = KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors=3)

model.fit(x_train, y_train)

prediction = model.predict(x_test)

accuracy = accuracy_score(y_test, prediction)

print("Confusion matrix : \n", confusion_matrix(y_test, prediction))
print("Rigth prediction : ", accuracy)
print("Wrong prediction : ", 1 - accuracy)

6/28/23, 10:34 - Venkataraman: import numpy as np
grid_world = np.array([[0, 0, 0],
                      [0, 1, 0],
                      [0, 0, 2]])
q_table = np.zeros((3, 3, 4))
learning_rate = 0.1
discount_factor = 0.9
num_episodes = 10

for episode in range(num_episodes):
    state = (0, 0)  # starting state
    while state != (2, 2):  # while the goal is not reached
        # Choose an action based on the epsilon-greedy policy
        if np.random.rand() < 0.1:
            action = np.random.randint(4)  # random action
        else:
            action = np.argmax(q_table[state])

        # Take the action and observe the next state and reward
        next_state = tuple(np.add(state, [(0, -1), (0, 1), (-1, 0), (1, 0)][action]))

        # Check if the next state is valid or out of bounds
        if next_state[0] < 0 or next_state[0] >= grid_world.shape[0] or next_state[1] < 0 or next_state[1] >= grid_world.shape[1]:
            # Out of bounds, assign a negative reward and stay in the current state
            reward = -1
            next_state = state
        else:
            reward = -1 if grid_world[next_state] == 0 else 10

        # Update the Q-value for the current state-action pair
        q_table[state][action] += learning_rate * (reward + discount_factor * np.max(q_table[next_state]) - q_table[state][action])

        state = next_state  # move to the next state

    # Print the learned Q-table
    print(q_table)

6/30/23, 11:21 - Venkataraman: def linear_search(arr, target):
    for i in range(len(arr)):
        if arr[i] == target:
            return i  # Return the index if target is found
    return -1  # Return -1 if target is not found

# Example usage:
my_list = [4, 7, 2, 9, 1, 5]
target_value = 9
index = linear_search(my_list, target_value)

if index != -1:
    print(f"Target value {target_value} found at index {index}")
else:
    print("Target value not found")

7/23/23, 19:11 - Venkataraman: The datagram will be fragmented into 3 fragments:

Fragment 1: 1000 bytes
Identification number: 465
Fragment offset: 0
Flags: Don't fragment (DF)
Fragment length: 1000 bytes
Fragment 2: 1500 bytes
Identification number: 465
Fragment offset: 1000
Flags: More fragments (MF)
Fragment length: 1500 bytes
Fragment 3: 100 bytes
Identification number: 465
Fragment offset: 2500
Flags: More fragments (MF)
Fragment length: 100 bytes
The first fragment will be able to fit entirely within the first link, so it will not be fragmented. The second fragment will be larger than the MTU of the first link, so it will be fragmented into two fragments. The third fragment will be larger than the MTU of the second link, so it will be fragmented into one fragment.

The following table summarizes the parameters associated with each fragment:

Fragment	Identification number	Fragment offset	Flags	Fragment length
1	465	0	DF	1000 bytes
2	465	1000	MF	1500 bytes
3	465	2500	MF	100 bytes

1/8/24, 20:08 - Venkataraman: LDR
1/8/24, 20:08 - Venkataraman: int trig = 13;
int echo = 12;
int led = 8;

long dur = 0;
int cm = 0;
int inch = 0;

void setup()
{
  Serial.begin(9600);
  pinMode(trig, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(echo, INPUT);
  pinMode(led, OUTPUT);
}

void loop()
{
  digitalWrite(trig, LOW);
  digitalWrite(trig, HIGH);
  digitalWrite(trig, HIGH);
  
  dur = pulseIn(echo, HIGH);
  cm = dur*0.034/2;
  inch = dur*0.0133/2;
  
  if (inch<20){
  	digitalWrite(led, HIGH);
  }
  else{
  	digitalWrite(led, LOW);
  }
  if (inch<100){
    Serial.print("Inches: ");
    Serial.print(inch);
    Serial.print("cm: ");
    Serial.print(cm);
  }
  delay(50);
}

void setup() {
  // Step 3: Begin serial communication

  Serial.println("Connecting to WiFi network...");

1/8/24, 22:26 - Venkataraman: Exp 6. Temperature notification using Arduino
1/8/24, 22:26 - Venkataraman: int sensor = A0;
#include<LiquidCrystal.h>
LiquidCrystal lcd(12, 11, 5, 4, 3, 2); 
//int sensor = A0; // Assigning analog pin A1 to variable 'sensor'
float tempc;  //variable to store temperature in degree Celsius
float vout;  //temporary variable to hold sensor reading
void setup()
{
pinMode(sensor,INPUT); // Configuring pin A1 as input
Serial.begin(9600);
lcd.begin(16,2);  
  delay(500);
}
void loop() 
{
vout=analogRead(A0);
float prehum=(vout/5);

vout=(vout*5.00)/1024.0;  
tempc=(vout-0.5)*100; // Storing value in Degree Celsius

float humconst = (0.16/0.0062);
float humi = prehum - humconst;
float pretruehumconst = 0.00216*tempc;
float pretruehum = 1.0546 - pretruehumconst;
float truehum = humi/pretruehum;
//delay(1000);  
lcd.setCursor(0,0);
lcd.print("Degree C = ");
lcd.print(tempc);
lcd.setCursor(0,1);
lcd.print("Humidity%= ");
lcd.print(truehum);
delay(1000); //Delay of 1 second for ease of viewing in serial monitor
}

    Serial.begin(9600);
    pinMode(led, OUTPUT);
    pinMode(touch, INPUT);

        Serial.println("Touch detected");

        digitalWrite(led, LOW);
   delay(1000);
}

2/20/24, 22:30 - Venkataraman: def orangecap(d):
    players_scores = {}
   
    # Iterate through each match
    for match in d.values():
        # Iterate through each player's score in the match
        for player, score in match.items():
            if player in players_scores:
                players_scores[player] += score
            else:
                players_scores[player] = score
               
    # Find the player with the highest total score
    top_player = max(players_scores, key=players_scores.get)
    top_score = players_scores[top_player]
   
    return (top_player, top_score)


def clean_poly(poly):
    # Remove zero coefficient terms
    poly = [(coef, exp) for coef, exp in poly if coef != 0]
    # Sort terms in descending order of exponent
    poly.sort(key=lambda x: x[1], reverse=True)
    return poly


def addpoly(p1, p2):
    p1 = clean_poly(p1)
    p2 = clean_poly(p2)
   
    result = []
    while p1 and p2:
        coef1, exp1 = p1[0]
        coef2, exp2 = p2[0]
       
        if exp1 == exp2:
            # Add coefficients for terms with the same exponent
            result.append((coef1 + coef2, exp1))
            p1.pop(0)
            p2.pop(0)
        elif exp1 > exp2:
            result.append((coef1, exp1))
            p1.pop(0)
        else:
            result.append((coef2, exp2))
            p2.pop(0)
   
    # Add remaining terms
    result.extend(p1)
    result.extend(p2)
   
    return clean_poly(result)


def multpoly(p1, p2):
    p1 = clean_poly(p1)
    p2 = clean_poly(p2)
   
    result = []
    for coef1, exp1 in p1:
        for coef2, exp2 in p2:
            new_coef = coef1 * coef2
            new_exp = exp1 + exp2
            # Combine like terms by adding coefficients
            found = False
            for i, (old_coef, old_exp) in enumerate(result):
                if old_exp == new_exp:
                    result[i] = (old_coef + new_coef, old_exp)
                    found = True
                    break
            if not found:
                result.append((new_coef, new_exp))
   
    return clean_poly(result)

2/28/24, 10:18 - Venkataraman: Predictive Maintenance for Equipment Failure:
In this project, participants can develop a predictive maintenance model to predict equipment failure before it occurs. They can use historical sensor data from machines or equipment along with machine learning algorithms like regression or time-series forecasting to predict when maintenance is needed. This project involves tasks such as data preprocessing, feature engineering, model training, and validation. Participants can also explore techniques like anomaly detection to identify unusual patterns in the data indicative of impending failures
2/28/24, 10:22 - Venkataraman: Sentiment Analysis on Social Media Data:
Participants can develop a sentiment analysis model to analyze sentiment (positive, negative, neutral) in social media posts or comments. They can use machine learning techniques such as natural language processing (NLP) and deep learning to classify the sentiment of the text. This project can involve tasks like data collection, preprocessing, feature extraction, model training, and evaluation.

3/16/24, 22:11 - Venkataraman: def is_palindrome(s):
    return s == s[::-1]

def find_longest_palindrome_subword(input_word):
    length = len(input_word)
    max_length = 0
    result = ""
    
    for i in range(length):
        for j in range(i+1, length+1):
            sub = input_word[i:j]
            if is_palindrome(sub) and len(sub) > max_length:
                max_length = len(sub)
                result = sub
            elif is_palindrome(sub) and len(sub) == max_length:
                result = min(result, sub)
        
    return max_length, result

N = int(input())
given_word = input().strip()

palindrome_length, longest_palindrome = find_longest_palindrome_subword(given_word)
print(palindrome_length)
print(longest_palindrome)

3/30/24, 11:32 - Venkataraman: char** splitVowels(char *str) {
    char **result = (char**)malloc(2 * sizeof(char*));
    result[0] = (char*)malloc(strlen(str) * sizeof(char));
    result[1] = (char*)malloc(strlen(str) * sizeof(char));
    int vowelIndex = 0, consonantIndex = 0;
    for (int i = 0; str[i] != '\0'; i++) {
        if (str[i] == 'a' || str[i] == 'e' || str[i] == 'i' || str[i] == 'o' || str[i] == 'u' ||
            str[i] == 'A' || str[i] == 'E' || str[i] == 'I' || str[i] == 'O' || str[i] == 'U') {
            result[0][vowelIndex++] = str[i];
        } else {
            result[1][consonantIndex++] = str[i];
        }
    }
    result[0][vowelIndex] = '\0';
    result[1][consonantIndex] = '\0';
    return result;
}

3/30/24, 12:03 - Venkataraman: def replace_zeros(matrix):
    rows, cols = len(matrix), len(matrix[0])
    
    for i in range(rows):
        for j in range(cols):
            if matrix[i][j] == 0:
                # Find the maximum positive integer that can replace 0
                max_val = max(matrix[i]) + 1
                for k in range(rows):
                    max_val = max(max_val, matrix[k][j] + 1)
                matrix[i][j] = max_val
    
    # Check if the matrix still has strictly increasing order
    for i in range(rows):
        for j in range(1, cols):
            if matrix[i][j] <= matrix[i][j-1]:
                return -1
    
    for j in range(cols):
        for i in range(1, rows):
            if matrix[i][j] <= matrix[i-1][j]:
                return -1
    
    # Calculate the sum of all integers in the matrix
    total_sum = sum(sum(row) for row in matrix)
    return total_sum

# Read input matrix
R, C = map(int, input().split())
matrix = [list(map(int, input().split())) for _ in range(R)]

# Call the function and print the result
result = replace_zeros(matrix)
print(result)

When we push ourselves beyond our comfort zones, we embark on a journey of self-discovery and realization of our true potential. It's like diving into the unknown, where we unearth hidden strengths, capabilities, and resilience that we never knew existed within us.

Facing challenges is not just about overcoming obstacles; it's about learning and growing from the experience. Each challenge presents an opportunity for learning and self-improvement. When we encounter difficulties, we are forced to think critically, problem-solve, and explore new solutions. This process enhances our cognitive abilities and equips us with valuable skills that we can apply in various aspects of our lives.

Moreover, tackling challenges instills in us a sense of perseverance and determination. We learn to persevere in the face of adversity, to keep pushing forward even when the odds are stacked against us. This resilience is a crucial trait that enables us to navigate through life's ups and downs with courage and grace.

Beyond the personal growth aspect, challenging ourselves fosters creativity and innovation. When we push the boundaries of what we know and step into uncharted territory, we are forced to think outside the box and come up with new ideas and solutions. This creative process not only enhances our problem-solving skills but also fuels our imagination and ingenuity.

Furthermore, embracing challenges keeps our minds active and engaged in continuous learning. Just as physical exercise strengthens our muscles, mental challenges strengthen our cognitive faculties. By exposing ourselves to new experiences, concepts, and ideas, we stimulate our brains and expand our knowledge base. This lifelong learning mindset is essential for personal and intellectual growth.

Indeed, taking on challenges is akin to embarking on an exciting adventure. It adds excitement, spice, and zest to our lives, making the journey more fulfilling and rewarding. Each challenge we face becomes a chapter in our life story, filled with lessons learned, victories achieved, and memories cherished.

In conclusion, challenging ourselves is not only important but essential for personal growth, development, and fulfillment. It pushes us to strive for excellence, to reach beyond our perceived limitations, and to become the best versions of ourselves. So let's embrace challenges with open arms, knowing that each obstacle we overcome brings us one step closer to realizing our full potential and living a life of purpose and fulfillment.
